### 3. [Critical infrastructure targeted in new phase of war with Iran](https://edition.cnn.com/world/live-news/iran-war-us-israel-trump-03-08-26?post-id=cmmhwkgg8000a3d5ukmeftl9e)
**Source**: CNN International | **Date**: 2026-03-08T15:31:00+00:00

> Senior clerics selected Mojtaba Khamenei to succeed his father after another day of strikes across the Middle East. A senior Iranian official warns the war is entering a "new phase."
> • Iran’s new leader: Iran has named Mojtaba Khamenei the country’s next supreme leader, state media reports, cementing hardliners’ grip on power following the killing of his father Ayatollah Ali Khamenei. US President Donald Trump previously said Khamenei’s son would be an “unacceptable” selection, and warned a new leader won’t “last long” without Trump’s support.
> • US service member killed: A seventh US service member has died from wounds sustained in the war with Iran, US Central Command announced.
> • School strike: New video appears to confirm a US airstrike targeted a naval base next to an Iranian elementary school where more than 160 students were killed last month, adding to a body of evidence contradicting Trump’s recent claims casting blame on Iran.
> • New phase of the war: A senior Iranian official told CNN the Middle East conflict is entering a “new phase” and threatened retaliatory strikes on energy infrastructure across the region.
> The Israeli military said it has begun “wide-scale strikes” on Iranian regime targets in capital city Tehran and in “additional areas” in the country.
> A CNN team on the ground in Tehran heard a wave of strikes a short while ago.
> The Israeli military said a short while ago that it had carried out another wave of strikes in Tehran, hitting what it said was the headquarters of the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps’ aerospace force.
> The site was used for research purposes and satellite monitoring, according to the Israeli military.
> In addition, the IDF said it had struck dozens of other sites including around 50 bunkers that it said stored ammunition, as well as an IRGC compound and an internal security center.
> Iranian Foreign Minister Abbas Araghchi says “nobody knows” who will be next supreme leader amid reports that the body responsible has selected a replacement for Ayatollah Ali Khamenei.
> Speaking on NBC’s “Meet The Press” on Sunday, Araghchi said, “There are lots of rumors around, but we have to wait for the Assembly of Experts to convene and vote for the new Supreme Leader.”
> The Assembly is an 88-member body of senior clerics that selects Iran’s Supreme Leader. Its members are elected by Iranian voters.
> Araghchi said that until the assembly convenes, the interim leadership council is in charge, and all Iranian institutions are carrying out their work. Some observers say that a new leader can’t be appointed until the assembly meets in person.
> Araghchi was also asked about US President Donald Trump’s assertion that he must have a say in the election of new leadership for Iran.
> Nor would Iran accept Trump’s demand for unconditional surrender, he said.
> He said that in June last year, Iran had accepted a ceasefire, but this time there needed to be a permanent end to the war.
> “No one wants to continue this war,” Araghchi said, adding that Iran will continue to act in self-defense.
> He insisted Iran was not attacking its neighbors but rather US facilities and installations in Gulf Arab countries.
> The United States does not plan to strike Iran’s oil industry or other energy infrastructure sites, Energy Secretary Chris Wright told CNN’s Jake Tapper on “State of the Union.” But Israel has not demonstrated the same.
> “There are no plans to target Iran’s oil industry, natural gas industry or anything about their energy industry,” Wright said. “These are Israeli strikes. These are local fuel depots to fill up the gas tank in this neighborhood in Tehran. The US is targeting zero energy infrastructure.”
> Wright acknowledged the “air quality impacts” of the Israeli strikes on Iran’s oil storage sites, which Iranians have told CNN are “suffocating.” But he said “a few days of lower air quality in Tehran” is nothing compared to what the Iranian people suffered under the regime.
> Gas prices: Wright also attempted to tout the Trump administration’s success lowering energy costs for Americans, while faced with the fact that gas and diesel have spiked during the conflict. Diesel prices are rising higher than gas right now, CNN reported, given it was in shorter supply previously.
> “We’ve seen a dramatic decline in gasoline prices and diesel prices. Assume you will see it in electricity prices, as well,” Wright said, referring to previous administration efforts. “Gasoline today is still $1.50 a gallon cheaper than it was in the middle of Biden administration, but you’re right; we want it back below $3 a gallon, and it will be again before too long.”
> Pressed by Tapper what “too long” means, Wright said weeks — not months.
> This post has been updated with video from the interview.
> White House press secretary Karoline Leavitt described rising gas prices due to the Iran war as “short term disruption for the long-term gain.”
> “This is a short-term disruption for the long-term gain of taking out the rogue Iranian terrorist regime and finally ending their restriction of the free flow of energy in the Middle East and in the Strait of Hormuz,” Leavitt said.
> Energy Secretary Chris Wright told CNN’s Jake Tapper earlier that one oil tanker made it through the Strait of Hormuz “about 24 hours ago.” The passage typically sees 60 or more ships a day. Leavitt also said Trump is “tapping into our newfound market in Venezuela,” after the US captured the nation’s president, Nicolás Maduro, and Trump said he would seize the country’s oil reserves.
> US Central Command on today issued a warning to Iranian people that Iran was putting them at risk by using “heavily populated civilian areas” to launch drones and ballistic missiles, potentially making those areas military targets.
> “This dangerous decision risks the lives of all civilians in Iran since locations used for military purposes lose protected status and could become legitimate military targets under international law,” the release said.
> A US official said the US military has seen several instances of Iran taking mobile drone or ballistic missile launchers into densely populated civilian areas. The warning on Sunday specifically named Dezful, Esfahan, and Shiraz as cities where Iran has used crowded areas to launch drones or missile attacks from.
> The official stressed that the warning shouldn’t be understood to mean the US military is preparing to strike the named locations, but the release also specifies that the US “cannot guarantee” civilian safety in or near facilities being used for military purposes. The warning on Sunday was intended to let Iran know “we see what you’re doing,” the official said, and to tell the Iranian people “your government is doing this to you, they’re putting you at risk.”
> “US forces strongly urge civilians in Iran to stay at home,” the release said. “The Iranian regime is knowingly endangering innocent lives.”
> A CNN team has witnessed the thick black plumes of smoke emerging from the fire at the Sharan Oil Deposit in northeast Tehran, one of Iran’s largest fuel storage facilities.
> We saw flames emerging from several storage tanks, still ablaze hours after overnight airstrikes.
> The surrounding area has also suffered extensive damage as a result, with workers scrambling to try and restore access to electricity.
> As we’ve reported, Tehran’s nearly 10 million people awoke to a morning overcast by thick black clouds after Israeli strikes hit Iran’s oil storage sites. Falling rainwater was black and saturated with oil.
> CNN operates in Iran only with government permission.
> Republican Sen. Thom Tillis said President Donald Trump owes the American people and Congress “clarity” on the operation in Iran.
> “I think that we need to articulate our objectives and our goals, because the American people right now are questioning whether or not we should be in this war,” he told CNN’s Jake Tapper on “State of the Union.”
> Tillis said clarity on those aspects of the conflict would allow them to “start building support for an engagement that could go on for more than the two months” under the War Powers Resolution.
> Tillis said if the conflict went beyond the two months, then the president would have to come to Congress.
> “I think beyond that 60-day period within the War Powers resolution, there has to either be a very clear commitment long term and a request in Congress, or, I believe, a cessation of hostilities,” he said.
> Oman, the US-allied Gulf Arab nation that mediated several rounds of talks between Washington and Tehran, has warned that the Middle East stands at a “dangerous turning point” because of the war with Iran and the dominance of the logic of force over dialogue.
> Omani Foreign Minister Badr Albusaidi made the statements to a virtual meeting of the Arab ministers, state-run Oman News Agency reported, noting that the political, economic, and security repercussions left “by this illegal war” threaten the region’s stability and the safety of its peoples.
> Just hours before Israel and the US struck Iran last week, Albusaidi said a deal between the US and Iran was “within our reach.”
> A wave of intense overnight strikes by Israel has disrupted fuel distribution in Tehran, according to the governor of the Iranian capital. CNN teams on the ground reported from a burning fuel depot and spoke to Iranians about oily rainfall in the city.
> Meanwhile, Iran struck a desalination plant in Bahrain. While local authorities said water supplies have not been disrupted, the conflict has brought into focus the critical role of desalination facilities in the Gulf.
> The death toll from US and Israeli strikes in Iran and Lebanon has once again increased, with the civilian death toll in Iran now surpassing that of last year’s 12-day war between the US, Israel and Iran.
> Catch up on more of the latest developments below:
> CNN’s Tim Lister, Ibrahim Dahman, Tamar Michaelis, Tal Shalev and Billy Stockwell contributed to this reporting.
> Despite earlier suggestions that the selection of a new supreme leader in Iran has been decided, hours later no public statement has been made by the body responsible for choosing a successor to Ayatollah Ali Khamenei.
> Some senior clerics in the 88-member Assembly of Experts have complained about the delay.
> One member, Ayatollah Abdullah Kayvani, demanded the process of “introducing the full-fledged jurist selected by the Assembly of Experts” be expedited.
> Another, Ayatollah Javad Ja’fari, said that the delay in introducing the new leader was a bitter and unwanted issue and accelerating the process was a serious demand of the people, according to the semi-official Mehr news agency.
> Rahim Tavakol, another representative on the Assembly, said its members had fulfilled their duty and “will soon announce the successor” to Ali Khamenei, who was killed in an Israeli airstrike on Tehran eight days ago.
> Some experts have speculated that the delay in the announcement is because the Assembly of Experts has been unable to physically meet amid the threat of US-Israeli strikes.
> Mohsen Borhani, a lawyer at the University of Tehran – posted on X that according to the Iranian constitution and the Assembly’s own regulations, at least two-thirds of its members must conduct deliberations and consultation in person.
> Several hardline members of the Assembly had earlier told state media that a decision has been made. But none has named the candidate chosen.
> Ukraine’s drone experts will be “on site” in the Middle East next week, President Volodymyr Zelensky said Sunday, as he suggested that resource-sharing with the US can be a “two-way street.”
> The Ukrainian leader made the comments after he was asked whether Ukraine would be able to help the US and its allies in the Gulf to repel Iranian drones.
> “Next week, when the experts are on site, they will look at the situation and help, because they are coming with their capabilities,” Zelensky said during a press conference with the Dutch prime minister, Ukrainian news agency Unian reported.
> Zelensky said on Wednesday that Gulf leaders and the US had asked for Ukraine’s help in repelling Iranian-made Shahed drones, which have become a signature weapon of Russia’s war on Ukraine.
> Officials in Kyiv have signaled that they can share their know-how on combating drones with states in the Middle East amid the current conflict.
> “If we are talking about increasing certain resources, we would very much like this to be a two-way street,” Zelensky said, in an apparent reference to receiving US air defense missiles.
> “You know what resources we are lacking, and we understand what resources the Gulf countries are lacking.”
> The most dangerous phase of the Iran war is likely to end soon, even if the war itself lasts longer, a UAE official told CNN, warning that it could take decades to rebuild trust between Tehran and the Arab states it has targeted.
> Relations between Iran and the Gulf Arab states it has been targeting will eventually normalize “because, at the end of the day, you are neighbors. But (the war) creates a huge trust gap that, in my opinion, will last for decades.”
> The official added that any new negotiated settlement with Iran would have to address its missile program. “Missiles are now center stage, because missiles now are not seen as self-defense.”
> The UAE, a regional business and tourism hub that relies heavily on foreign workers, has been among the hardest hit of the roughly dozen countries that have reported being struck by Iranian projectiles.
> The official said that France is helping patrol the country’s skies and intercept missiles and drones, and added that the country had been preparing for an emergency “for a long time” and has enough food stocks.
> Iran is using 60% of its firepower to attack US bases and “strategic interests” in the region, the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC) spokesperson said a short while ago.
> “About 60% of our offensive capability – our missile fire and drone operations – is directed at the bases and strategic interests of the United States in the region, and 40% is directed at the targets of the Israeli regime,” Ali Mohammad Naeini said in a televised address.
> Iran has shot down 80 drones launched towards it, Naeini said, including “three giant, highly advanced American MQ-9 drones” and 74 Israeli drones.
> The United Arab Emirates has borne the brunt of Iranian projectiles fired at Arab states, detecting more than 230 ballistic missiles and more than 1,400 drones since the conflict began, its defense ministry said.
> At least 394 people have been killed in Lebanon since Israel began strikes on the country earlier this week, according to the Lebanese health ministry.
> Among those killed are 83 children and 42 women, health minister Rakan Nasreddine said. At least another 1,130 people have been injured in the same time frame, he added, including 254 children and 274 women.
> Israel says it is launching attacks on its northern neighbor to eradicate the Iran-backed militant group Hezbollah.
> More than 450,000 people inside Lebanon have been registered as displaced as of yesterday, the country’s social affairs minister Haneen Sayed said at a news conference.
> CNN’s Fred Pleitgen and photojournalist Claudia Otto spoke to Iranians at gas stations in Tehran after fuel supplies in the capital were disrupted overnight by a fresh wave of Israeli attacks targeting energy resources.
> CNN operates in Iran only with government permission.
> Two Israeli soldiers were killed in southern Lebanon early Sunday morning, according to the Israeli military, marking the country’s first military deaths since the war began.
> The soldiers were near a D-9 bulldozer when Hezbollah fired an anti-tank missile at their position, an Israeli source told CNN.
> The Israel Defense Forces (IDF) identified one of those killed as MSgt. Maher Khatar, 38. The IDF did not identify the second soldier killed.
> At least 14 Israeli soldiers have been injured in fighting across the Israel-Lebanon border
> Five people were injured in central Israel in an Iranian ballistic missile attack, according to Israel’s emergency response service.
> Magen David Adom (MDA) said one man in Tel Aviv was in serious condition. Yechezkel Goldreich, a paramedic with MDA who responded to the scene, said he found “frightened people lying on the grass and a man around 40 years old who was suffering from a severe shrapnel injury.”
> According to Israel’s Fire and Rescue Authority, the missile used a cluster warhead, spreading submunitions across parts of central Israel, including Tel Aviv and Petah Tikva.
> The Israeli military said search and rescue teams were operating at multiple impact sites.
> Israel has intercepted the majority of ballistic missile launches from Iran, but shrapnel from the missiles has rained down across the country. Israel has also accused Iran of launching cluster munitions, and CNN teams in Tel Aviv have observed what appear to be cluster warheads splitting up in the skies over central Israel.
> Countries across the Persian Gulf reported a new wave of drone and missile strikes on Sunday morning.
> The Kuwaiti Army said that a “wave of hostile drones” targeted fuel storage at the Kuwait International Airport on Sunday local time, and that shrapnel and debris from interceptions had damaged some civilian infrastructure. The armed forces also intercepted a number of ballistic missiles, state media reported.
> Kuwait’s Public Institution for Social Security building was hit in a drone strike, according to state-run media. Footage geolocated by CNN showed the roughly 22-story building in flames in the early hours. State media said there were no injuries.
> Two Kuwaiti border security personnel were killed on Sunday morning “while performing their national duty,” state media reported, without giving other details. It’s not clear if the incident was related to the strikes at the airport and government building.
> Saudi Arabia’s Ministry of Defense has also said it endured drone attacks, intercepting at least 21 drones in the wee hours of Sunday morning.
> Across the King Fahd Causeway in Bahrain, the Interior Ministry said three people were wounded and a university building was damaged by falling “missile fragments.” A water desalination plant was also damaged, it said. But in statement to CNN the Electricity and Water Authority said the attack had no impact on water supplies or water network capacity.
> Air defenses in the UAE were “currently responding to a missile threat,” the National Emergency Crisis and Disaster Management Authority wrote in a post on X early Sunday.
> Later, the country’s defense ministry said its air defenses had detected 17 ballistic missiles and 117 drones so far today. Of those, 16 missiles and 113 drones were intercepted, while one missile fell into the sea, and four drones fell within UAE territory.
> The various drone and missile attacks come after Iran’s President Masoud Pezeshkian apologized to Gulf nations on Saturday for the many drone and missile attacks of the past week on US bases in the region, saying Iran would stop striking its neighbors unless it came under attack.
> Pezeshkian’s office later clarified that he meant “if regional countries do not cooperate in America’s attack on us, we will not attack them.”
> This post has been updated with additional information. CNN’s Ibrahim Dahman contributed to this reporting.
> The black rain that has fallen on Iran’s capital may be “dangerous” to people’s health, a weather expert told CNN, as the Red Crescent warned residents to stay indoors until the air had fully cleared.
> CNN’s team on the ground in Tehran filmed thick black clouds and showers of oil-saturated rain on Sunday morning, hours after strikes on oil installations and refineries.
> The Red Crescent urged people to stay indoors even after the rain stopped, saying the air could become hazardous to breathe as acidic droplets evaporated into the atmosphere.
> Residents of the capital should use a saline solution to rinse their throats and nasal passages of harmful soot particles, it said in a social media post.
> “Breathing in pollutants of this kind can result in both long- and short-term respiratory conditions… and can actually lead to cancer in the longer term,” said Jim Dale, senior meteorologist from British Weather Services.
> Black rain can cause crops to be “blighted” as well as triggering serious health consequences, Dale warned, adding, “it is dangerous.”
> Tehran was already heavily polluted before the latest explosions. A 2018 World Health Organization report classed Tehran as one of the world’s most polluted cities, with over 4,000 deaths a year due to air pollution.
> “[Black rain] it’s the fallout of war… And often it’s not thought about… It’s, you know, you bomb this. And then suddenly, suddenly, the entire nation has a problem in terms of feeding themselves, or the medical side of things,” Dale told CNN.

### 4. [Derby in DR Congo match abandoned amid witchcraft claims](https://www.maravipost.com/derby-in-dr-congo-match-abandoned-amid-witchcraft-claims/)
**Source**: The Maravi Post | **Date**: 2026-03-08T19:38:00+00:00

> LUMBASHI-(MaraviPost)-A highly anticipated derby between TP Mazembe and FC Saint Eloi Lupopo was abandoned on Sunday,March 08,2026 after violent clashes erupted among supporters during the match in Lubumbashi.
> The match, played at Stade TP Mazembe, had reached halftime with the score at 0-0 when tensions began to rise in the stands.
> Supporters from the rival teams reportedly started arguing, which quickly escalated into violence.
> According to reports from the stadium, the chaos was triggered by disagreements among fans over alleged use of traditional “black magic” or spiritual practices believed by some supporters to influence football matches.
> Objects were thrown onto the pitch as sections of the crowd became uncontrollable.
> The situation forced players and match officials to run back to the dressing rooms while security personnel attempted to restore order.
> With the environment becoming increasingly unsafe, the referee decided to abandon the game before the second half could begin, bringing the much anticipated derby to a premature end.
> The rivalry between TP Mazembe and FC Saint Eloi Lupopo is one of the fiercest in Congolese football. Both clubs are based in Lubumbashi and their encounters often attract thousands of passionate supporters.
> Historically, matches between the two sides have sometimes been marred by crowd trouble.
> One of the most tragic incidents occurred during a derby in 2001 when panic in the stadium led to a deadly stampede that claimed several lives.
> Beliefs surrounding witchcraft and spiritual influence have occasionally surfaced in football across the Democratic Republic of the Congo.
> In some cases, players or teams have been accused by rival supporters of using supernatural means to gain an advantage during matches.
> A well known example occurred during the Linafoot competitions in the past, when fans invaded the pitch after accusing a player of using witchcraft to affect the outcome of a game.
> Such beliefs, though widely debated, remain part of football folklore in parts of Central Africa and have occasionally fueled tensions between rival supporters.
> Sunday’s incident once again highlighted the intensity surrounding one of the country’s biggest football rivalries and raised concerns about fan behavior and stadium safety.

### 5. [The precedent Edgar Lungu set: A tale of leadership, legacy, burial disputes](https://www.maravipost.com/the-precedent-edgar-lungu-set-a-tale-of-leadership-legacy-burial-disputes/)
**Source**: The Maravi Post | **Date**: 2026-03-08T09:11:00+00:00

> LUSAKA-(MaraviPost)-The passing of Edgar Lungu, former president of Zambia, and the ongoing dispute surrounding his burial, presents a deeply poignant reflection on political legacies, family rights, and national symbolism.
> It is a striking example of how the actions of a leader in life can come full circle in death, highlighting the delicate balance between personal wishes, family sovereignty, and the state’s prerogative in honoring national figures.
> At the heart of this unfolding drama is the precedent that Lungu himself established during his presidency—a precedent that now defines his own fate.
> When Kenneth Kaunda, Zambia’s first president and a revered founding father, died in June 2021, the nation watched closely as his burial arrangements became contested.
> Kaunda’s family wished for a private burial at his farm, beside his late wife, reflecting a desire for intimate remembrance and personal dignity.
> However, Edgar Lungu, then president, refused this request, insisting that Kaunda be laid to rest at the national Heroes’ Acre—a site reserved for honoring former presidents and national heroes.
> This insistence was grounded in the state’s interest in commemorating Kaunda’s monumental contribution to Zambia’s independence and nationhood in a public and symbolic manner.
> The legal battle that ensued ended with the High Court ruling in favor of the government, reinforcing the state’s authority in such matters.
> This moment was pivotal, as it established a clear standard: the burial of a former president, no matter the family’s wishes, would be a state affair, publicly commemorated at the national site.
> Fast forward to June 2025. Edgar Lungu himself passes away, and the very precedent he set is now the source of a bitter dispute over his own burial.
> His body remains in a mortuary in South Africa, delayed by a standoff between his family and the Zambian government.
> The family advocates for a private burial, honoring Lungu’s personal and familial wishes away from the public eye.
> Conversely, the government insists that Lungu be buried at the national burial site, as per the precedent, to publicly honor his service to the nation.
> Complicating matters further is the family’s request that the current president not attend the funeral, signaling deep personal and political fractures that underscore the tensions between private grief and public ceremony.
> This ironic and heartrending situation serves as a powerful reminder of the double-edged nature of leadership decisions and the precedents they set.
> Edgar Lungu, in his enforcement of a state-controlled burial for Kenneth Kaunda, effectively laid down a rule that now governs even his own posthumous treatment.
> The phrase “what goes around comes around” resonates here with profound intensity.
> Lungu’s insistence on a national burial site for Kaunda was motivated by a desire to preserve the dignity and unity of the nation’s historical memory, yet it has also led to his own family’s distress and a protracted delay in his burial.
> The significance of this situation extends beyond personal tragedy or political rivalry.
> It raises fundamental questions about the role of the state in managing the legacies of its leaders, the rights of families to mourn privately, and the symbolism that burial sites carry for national identity.
> When a leader’s life is intertwined with the nation’s history, their death becomes a public event, a moment of collective memory and reflection.
> However, the family’s perspective—rooted in intimacy, personal loss, and respect for the deceased’s wishes—cannot be underestimated or sidelined without consequence. The clash between these two imperatives is starkly illustrated in the Lungu-Kaunda burial saga.
> Edgar Lungu’s role as the “pace setter” in this context is undeniable. By enforcing a national burial for Kenneth Kaunda, he established a benchmark that the government is now applying uniformly.
> This consistency is important for maintaining a sense of order and respect for the office of the presidency.
> Yet, it also demonstrates how precedents, once set, can bind even those who create them, sometimes in ways they might not have anticipated or desired.
> This is a lesson in the unforeseen consequences of policy decisions, especially those touching on matters as sensitive as death and mourning.
> It is essential to view this situation with a balanced lens.On one hand, the government’s insistence on a national burial site honors the office and the nation’s history.
> It reflects an understanding that former presidents are public figures whose legacies belong to the people, warranting public recognition and commemoration.
> This approach can foster national unity and pride, reminding citizens of their shared heritage and the sacrifices made by their leaders.
> On the other hand, the family’s desire for a private burial is equally valid.
> It reflects a universal human need to grieve and say farewell in a manner that feels personal and respectful of the deceased’s individual identity.
> The family’s exclusion of the current president from the funeral further highlights the complex interpersonal and political dynamics that often accompany such events. Ignoring these human elements risks deepening divisions and causing additional pain to those closest to the departed.
> In the end, the path forward requires wisdom, empathy, and compromise.
> The precedent Edgar Lungu set is powerful and clear, but it must be applied with sensitivity to the unique circumstances surrounding his own death.
> The government and Lungu’s family need to engage in open dialogue, striving to honor both the national significance of Lungu’s legacy and the personal rights of his loved ones.
> This balance is crucial for healing and for preserving the dignity of all involved.
> The story of Edgar Lungu’s burial dispute is more than a political controversy; it is a mirror reflecting the complexities of leadership, legacy, and the human experience of loss.
> It reminds us that the decisions leaders make do not end with their tenure but ripple forward, shaping not only history but also their own final chapters.
> As Zambia navigates this difficult moment, it is an opportunity to reflect on how best to honor those who have served the nation, while respecting the deeply personal nature of death and mourning.
> Edgar Lungu’s legacy, shaped in life and now tested in death, challenges us all to consider how we balance the public and private, the collective and the intimate, in the stories we tell about our leaders—and ourselves.
